    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_122131345" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/122131345" />
    <a title="Lois" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2355848-lois"><img alt="Lois" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506004035p2/2355848.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/122131345?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lois" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lois" href="/user/show/2355848-lois">Lois</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780380009473" href="/book/show/76671.The_Dollmaker">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer122131345" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1272446603850883485">I am having a hard time thinking of a single emotion that this book did not drag out of me. It seemed to elicit them all. Hope, happiness, worry, anger, heartbreak...on and on. <br/><br/>I saw the made-for-TV movie years ago and have always remembered it. Mostly the main character, Gertie, and one of her daughters, Cassie, along with Cassie’s friend, Callie Lou. Some of the other characters and plot lines faded a bit in my mind over the years, but I would sometimes think of these two: of Gertie’s brave de</span>
  <span id="freeText1272446603850883485" style="display:none">I am having a hard time thinking of a single emotion that this book did not drag out of me. It seemed to elicit them all. Hope, happiness, worry, anger, heartbreak...on and on. <br /><br />I saw the made-for-TV movie years ago and have always remembered it. Mostly the main character, Gertie, and one of her daughters, Cassie, along with Cassie’s friend, Callie Lou. Some of the other characters and plot lines faded a bit in my mind over the years, but I would sometimes think of these two: of Gertie’s brave determination and Cassie’s fanciful imagination. <br /><br />I can’t believe I never even thought to look up the book before, and am so happy that someone decided to donate their copy to a book fair last September. But if I knew back then how much I would totally love this book, I’d have sat down to start reading it the same night I bought it, rather than letting it sit on my shelf for 5 months. <br /><br />In my opinion, its a wonderful book. I dreaded getting to the one part I especially can’t mention here, because it was something that I remembered so vividly from the movie. It made me cry when I saw it then, and I knew that reading it would be just as, or possibly even more, heartbreaking. It was. I cried just as hard, all over again. The tragedy that happens after you get to know these people so well, and after they’ve already been through so much, is hard to believe, and you can’t imagine how they will go on afterwards, especially one of them. There were a few parts of the book that made me want to cry, but this one really did it.<br /><br />The characters are so real. Gertie is a strong, brave, hardworking woman who tries to do what she thinks is right, but can’t always. Her husband Clovis, while basically a good man, simply doesn’t understand his nature-loving wife and the simple things that could so easily make her happy. The choices they make, and those that are made for them, to improve their lives, just don’t always work out that way, and often make things much worse. There was one scene, in particular, when I wanted to scream at Clovis and just shake him, when he suggested that maybe Gertie should have done something that would have made things better for their family. He had no clue it was the one thing she had been ready to do, the one thing she wanted more than anything to do, and that he was the one who had caused her to not do it, which changed their lives so much for the worse.<br /><br />I felt for all of them. How much more could Gertie put up with, as her spirit continued to be beaten down, without completely breaking? I worried for their oldest son, Reuben, who was so much like his mother, and began to turn against her when things got out of her control, then finally decided for himself how his life was going to be. Sweet little Cassie meant so well, but just couldn’t fit in except on her own terms, and her parents talking against her best (and only) friend made her afraid to be who she really was and caused her so much unhappiness.<br /><br />The writing is rich and beautiful. When Gertie is thinking of her Kentucky home and how things looked and felt and smelled ... the natural lighting she lived by which made clocks unnecessary .. .the sound of the rain on the roof and the smell of it in the wind ... her connection to the animals she tended ... the feel of the knife in her hand as she did her beloved whittling, turning chunks of wood into things they needed, like handles for tools, but also dolls and birds and holy figures that she called her “foolishness” (except for a special piece that she always turned to work on for comfort) .... many of these descriptions I read over more than once.<br /><br />The story is a hard one, and often very sad, but still uplifting in a way, and I am so happy to own a copy of this book. I wanted to read it quickly to find out what happened, but I also wanted to read slowly, to make it last a little longer. It is definitely a favorite.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1272446603850883485" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YovqBwTeqhBBrTqT7X8CpFMsMq0gs3AnETyZaUwxlf5eArGMDGCNv0m8PNMxxhJ0k+BRWseoWXSXof2tD4UeaA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6dd0TDFklpcuv3gnu2tmv6VcvJuuBjl6N8tVaVu9jIrVXi/HOdqxOCaufmdn0nZvZZDfbEkdECmxVjGtGAkHHA==')})
  }

Emma Bell Miles, however, had two advantages that most of the writers lacked: her familiarity with the region that lent insight and subtlety to her analysis; and a consciously dual perspective on her own life and the lives of mountain people gave her writing a generous scope and fine balance. First published in 1905, The Spirit of the Mountains remains one of the few books about Appalachia that neither romanticizes nor condescends not depends on the unconscious acceptance of middle-class, mainstream American values for its analysis.\n\nAlthough Miles lived on Walden\'s Ridge in the southern Cumberlands with her husband and family, her work as a writer and painter involved her with the wealthy society people of Chattanooga and she was a perceptive observer of the complex interplay between mountain life and life elsewhere. Her feelings about both were ambivalent; however, her intelligence, integrity, and honesty made her acutely conscious of the strengths and weaknesses as well as the coherence and contradictions of both mountain people and those of the city. This intimate awareness of the cultural and social fabric of mountain life reveals itself in the book\'s skillful and realistic portrayal of the music, religion, traditions, and lore of the mountains.\n\nPublisher: New York, J.
